    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b64dda478f6144f79f0458c0caf38360">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd823d033-a093-49e6-a99f-e8d8f5d47cfd'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d35498341" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>MoS<sub>2</sub> is widely reported as anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, its ability to operate effectively across a wide temperature range and at high rates continues to pose fundamental challenges, limiting its further development. Herein, a monolayer Fe-doped MoS<sub>2</sub>/N,O-codoped C overlapping structure is designed and employed as an anode for wide-temperature-range SIBs. Fe doping imparts MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode with zero bandgap characteristics, an increased interlayer spacing, and low sodium-ion diffusion energy barriers across wide operation temperatures. Impressively, Fe atoms doped into the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice can be reduced to superparamagnetic Fe<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals of ≈2&nbsp;nm during conversion reactions. In situ magnetometry reveals that these Fe<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals can be used as electron acceptor in the formation of space charge zones with Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby triggering strong spin-polarized surface capacitance that facilitates fast sodium-ion storage over a wide temperature range. Consequently, the designed MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode demonstrates exceptional fast-charging capability in half/full cells operating at −40–60 °C. This study provides novel perspectives on the utilization of heteroatom doping strategies in conversion-type electrode material design and proves the effectiveness of spin-polarized surface capacitance effect on enhancing sodium-ion storage over a wide temperature range.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404263-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404263-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which have a similar operating principle to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), have been widely investigated and considered potential alternatives to LIBs due to the abundant reserves of sodium and its low price.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0001_R_d35498333e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As is well known to us, seasonal and daily operating temperature fluctuations would greatly affect the fast-charging performance and energy density of rechargeable batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0002_R_d35498333e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0003_R_d35498333e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, LIBs will lose most of their capacity when they operate at temperatures lower than −40&nbsp;°C, and they are basically unable to obtain good fast-charging performance at low temperatures due to lithium plating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0004_R_d35498333e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, for the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å), a slower diffusion rate into the electrode materials would result in worse fast-charging performance, especially at low temperatures. As reported, when SIBs operate below 0 °C, the kinetics of sodium ion diffusion decelerate even further, leading to a reduction in rate capability, capacity, and energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0005_R_d35498333e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the exploration of fast-charging SIBs, which can operate at a wide temperature range, still faces a huge challenge. One of the key factors affecting the wide-temperature-range fast-charging performance of SIBs, especially at low temperatures, is whether sodium ions can be rapidly diffused into electrode materials, especially anode materials. Hard carbon as first-generation SIB anode material still faces huge challenges at low temperatures, such as low capacity, poor rate performance, unsatisfactory cycling stability, and safety issues, which severely impede the practical application of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0006_R_d35498333e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the development of high-rate-capable and high-capacity anode materials along with long cycling life and high safety for SIBs capable of operating across a wide temperature range is of great significance, but still is a huge challenge.</p>
         
         <p>MoS<sub>2</sub>, a typical representative of 4d transition metal sulfides, has been a focus as SIBs anode ascribed to its high theoretical capacity (670 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and large interlayer spacing (0.62&nbsp;nm) in crystal plane of (002).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0007_R_d35498333e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the poor electrical conductivity (EC) and huge volume change on cycling lead to bad electrochemical performances, such as rapid capacity decay and poor rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0008_R_d35498333e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Tremendous efforts including the preparation of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanostructures and MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composites have been made to address the above-mentioned challenges.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0009_R_d35498333e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nanostructured MoS<sub>2</sub> can decrease ion diffusion length and accommodate the strain, but the agglomeration of nanoparticles and the increased interface charge transfer resistance result in a limited improvement in capacity fading and poor rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0010_R_d35498333e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composites, such as sandwiched nanoarchitecture,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0011_R_d35498333e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Res.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> core-shell nanostructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0012_R_d35498333e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub>/carbon nanocomposite,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0013_R_d35498333e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Res.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and layer-by-layer stacked MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanostructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0014_R_d35498333e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been designed, which show huge advantages in inhibiting the capacity decay and enhancing rate capability. Among them, the layer-by-layer stacked MoS<sub>2</sub>-C nanostructure can increase ion storage sites, promote the transport of ions in the interlayer, and suppress the volume change of MoS<sub>2</sub>, thereby leading to an improvement in capacity and rate capability. However, such engineering also has the following limitations: 1) The intrinsic EC of MoS<sub>2</sub> is not radically improved, which severely hampers electron transfer within MoS<sub>2</sub>; 2) With the interlayer distance between carbon and MoS<sub>2</sub> reduced to 0.48&nbsp;nm from the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> distance of 0.62&nbsp;nm, Na-ion diffusion in the interlayer is undeniably hindered, adversely affecting rate capability; 3) The presence of a significant amount of low-capacity carbon unavoidably reduces the overall capacity of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-C composite.</p>
         
         <p>The heteroatom doping strategy, capable of fine-tuning the electronic structure and interlayer distance in layered transition metal sulfides, demonstrates significant potential in MoS<sub>2</sub>-based electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0015_R_d35498333e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, our recent reports have confirmed the existence of spin-polarized surface capacitance on the surfaces of superparamagnetic 3d transition metal nanoparticles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0016_R_d35498333e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This effect can impart 3d transition metal-based electrode materials with increased capacity and robust rate capability. Specifically, charges and electrons can be stored separately in the charge acceptor phase and 3d transition metal nanoparticles, and they can attract each other and accumulate at the interface double layer to form a space charge region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0017_R_d35498333e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Matter">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It seems reasonable to believe that introducing 3d transition metal atoms in a doped form into the layer-by-layer stacked MoS<sub>2</sub>-C structure (mentioned above) can not only adjust the electronic structure and interlayer distance of MoS<sub>2</sub>, but also, more significantly, once the doped 3d transition metal atoms form superparamagnetic nanocrystals during conversion reactions, there is a potential to induce spin-polarized surface capacitance that facilitates diffusion and storage of sodium ions across a wide temperature range. However, to the best of our knowledge, there have been scarce reports on the utilization of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes for sodium storage over wide operation temperatures, let alone the investigation of the spin-polarized surface capacitance effects in 3d transition metal atom-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in such a wide operation temperature range.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, magnetism plays a significant role in battery research. Previous reports have shown that studying the magnetism of electrode materials effectively promotes understanding of the structure, composition, and electrochemical properties of electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0018_R_d35498333e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For electrode materials composed of transition metal compounds, all electrochemical processes involve changes in the valency of transition metal ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0019_R_d35498333e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Magnetism is highly sensitive to changes in the valency of elements, as well as to variations in material structure and morphology during electrochemical processes. Therefore, magnetic measurements serve as a powerful tool for studying battery processes involving transition metals. They not only characterize the evolution of these elements during electrochemical processes but also analyze the reactions involved in electrochemical processes. Especially, magnetometry provides an important perspective for analyzing the structure and electrochemical processes of transition metal compound electrode materials from the standpoint of electron spins and magnetic interactions between redox-active species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0020_R_d35498333e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Energy Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We previously confirmed through in situ magnetometry that transition metal compound anodes exhibit spin-polarized surface capacitance effects in room-temperature LIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0016_R_d35498333e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0017_R_d35498333e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Matter">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0021_R_d35498333e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and aluminium-ion batteries (AIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0022_R_d35498333e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it remains unknown whether this effect exists in SIBs operating over a wide temperature range. In situ magnetometry, as a powerful real-time magnetic monitoring tool, can provide important assistance in filling this gap.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a unique monolayer Fe-doped MoS<sub>2</sub>/N, O-codoped C overlapping structure (denoted as Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC) is designed to enhance the capacity and the Na-ion transport capability of MoS<sub>2</sub> in SIBs across wide operation temperatures from −40 to 60&nbsp;°C. Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC with rich Fe doping sites, an expanded interlayer spacing (11.6 Å), an enhanced intrinsic EC, and low Na-ion diffusion energy barriers are uniformly embedded in NOC matrix to form the composite (named FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C), which has the following advantages: 1) Fe-doping enlarges interlayer distance and improves intrinsic EC of MoS<sub>2</sub>; 2) The unique Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC enables low sodium-ion diffusion barriers across a wide temperature range, revealed by density functional theory (DFT) calculations; 3) In situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and ex situ TEM observations demonstrate that the doped Fe atoms in MoS<sub>2</sub> lattices can be converted into superparamagnetic Fe<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals (≈2&nbsp;nm) during conversion reactions at −40–60&nbsp;°C; 4) Construction of the overlapping structure can effectively hinder the growth of transition metal nanocrystals during the conversion reaction, thus benefiting for obtaining ultrasmall Fe<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals; 5) In situ magnetometry reveals the presence of robust spin-polarized surface capacitance on surface of superparamagnetic Fe<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals at −40–60&nbsp;°C, supplying extra ion storage sites and boosting ion transport. Based on these features, the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanocomposite as anode material for SIBs displays high capacity exceeding theoretical capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>, fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability, and long cycling life in half and full cells operating at −40–60 °C.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404263-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404263-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404263-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404263-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Synthesis and Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the schematic diagram of the preparation process of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C samples. FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, and FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III are prepared by thermally decomposing varying quantities (0.4/0.36/0.54&nbsp;g, 0.6/0.28/0.42&nbsp;g, and 0.8/0.2/0.3&nbsp;g, respectively) of 2(C<sub>11</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>).Fe /(NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub>/CH<sub>3</sub>C(O)N(CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> within sealed containers. With increasing temperature, (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> gradually undergoes thermal decomposition and transforms into MoS<sub>2</sub>. Simultaneously, this process involves the introduction of Fe doping into MoS<sub>2</sub> (Fe atoms originating from 2(C<sub>11</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>).Fe). Under high vapor pressure, the pyrolysis products of CH<sub>3</sub>C(O)N(CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and (C<sub>11</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>−</sup> situated between the layers of MoS<sub>2</sub> can transform into single-layer carbon co-doped with both N and O, thus completing the construction of Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC. Simultaneously, these pyrolysis products surrounding Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC can be transformed into NOC matrix to obtain FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C. To prove the advantage of Fe doping, we prepare undoped MoS<sub>2</sub>/C by thermally decomposing 0.6&nbsp;g of (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> and 0.9&nbsp;g of CH<sub>3</sub>C(O)N(CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, pure MoS<sub>2</sub> is synthesized through the thermal decomposition of 1.5&nbsp;g of (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> for comparative purposes.</p>
            
            <p>Clearly, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information), FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information), and FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information) display a flower-like morphology assembled by nanosheets, while FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III exhibits a morphology that consists of both flower-like and randomly stacked nano-sheets (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d–f</a>, Supporting Information). Differently, pure MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits an irregular morphology with micron-sized bulk particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2g</a>, Supporting Information), which remains the morphology of pristine (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2h</a>, Supporting Information). TEM observation shows that pure MoS<sub>2</sub> has a clear lamellar structure with a standard interlayer distance of 6.2 Å and displays typical diffraction rings of (002), (100), and (110) crystal planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS: 37–1492) in selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a–c</a>, Supporting Information). After forming the monolayer MoS<sub>2</sub> and C overlapping structure (MoS<sub>2</sub>/C), the interlayer distance of MoS<sub>2</sub> increases to 9.6 Å due to the insertion of monolayer C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3d,e</a>, Supporting Information), which is also confirmed by the SAED pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3f</a>, Supporting Information) that the d-spacing of (002) plane expands to 9.6 Å. Upon the introduction of Fe doping, the interlayer distance of MoS<sub>2</sub> continues to increase from 9.6 Å (MoS<sub>2</sub>/C) to 10.7 Å (FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) and can even reach 11.6 Å (FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1a</a>). This phenomenon may be attributed to strong electrostatic repulsion generated by Fe doping within the interlayers of MoS<sub>2</sub> (detailed discussion in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5–S8</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). However, with excessive Fe doping, the formation of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S results in a slight reduction in the interlayer distance to 10.9 Å (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) image shows numerous Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOCs with a clear lamellar structure of ≈5 layers distributed in carbon matrix (marked in Figure <a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1a</a>) to form the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C sample. Importantly, the obvious atomic layer can be observed between the adjacent MoS<sub>2</sub> layers (marked by white arrows, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), providing substantial support for the construction of a monolayer overlapping structure. SAED pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1c</a>) reveals diffraction rings corresponding to the (002), (100), and (110) planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS: 37–1492) with respective d-spacing values of 11.6, 2.7, and 1.6 Å. High-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) images and energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings prove the homogeneous distribution of Mo, S, and Fe elements throughout the NOC matrix (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1d–j</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404263-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6cbdd989-0693-4178-b812-8099bb94748f/adfm202404263-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6cbdd989-0693-4178-b812-8099bb94748f/adfm202404263-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a5cb2a6b-a4ff-4249-a964-3616ae08db8c/adfm202404263-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6cbdd989-0693-4178-b812-8099bb94748f/adfm202404263-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404263-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404263" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM characterizations of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II: a,b) HRTEM images, (c) SAED pattern, d) HAADF image, and e–j) the corresponding EDS elemental mapping images, k) ADF image and corresponding EELS line scans for elements l) Mo, m) Fe, and n) C. Structural characterizations of the obtained samples: o) XRD patterns, p) high-resolution XPS spectra of Fe 2p, and q) EPR spectra.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We conduct electron energy-loss spectroscopy (EELS) analysis on FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II in line-scan mode (along the yellow arrow in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1k</a>) to investigate the composition of Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC. The obtained peak spacing of Mo and S elements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1l</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information, respectively) is ≈1.16&nbsp;nm, further demonstrating the enlarged interlayer spacing (≈1.16&nbsp;nm) of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Meanwhile, Fe peaks emerge at positions nearly identical to those of Mo peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1m</a>), which can be regarded as evidence in support that the Fe atoms replace Mo atoms to dope into the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattices. In addition, distinct carbon peaks are observed situated between the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1n</a>). Specifically, the three carbon peaks, centered at 1.22, 2.41, and 3.65&nbsp;nm, are situated between three sets of Mo peaks spanning from 0.64 to 1.79&nbsp;nm, 1.79 to 2.96&nbsp;nm, and 2.96 to 4.15&nbsp;nm, respectively. These results fully evidence the Fe doping of MoS<sub>2</sub> and the existence of single-layer carbon between MoS<sub>2</sub> layers.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1o</a>) show the diffraction peaks of the crystalline hexagonal structure of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS: 37–1492), which confirms the formation of MoS<sub>2</sub>. The diffraction peaks associated with (002) crystal planes in MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, and FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III are centered at 14.3°, 9.2°, 8.3°, 7.6°, and 8.0°, corresponding to the interlayer distances of 0.62, 0.96, 1.06, 1.16, and 1.10&nbsp;nm, respectively. This result is consistent with the HRTEM results mentioned above. However, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III shows three new peaks at 30.0°, 43.4°, and 53.2°, corresponding to (200), (2022), and (220) crystal planes of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S (JCPDS:29-0726), respectively. This confirms the formation of Fe<sub>1-x</sub>S, in good agreement with the HRTEM observation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The Raman spectra in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a> (Supporting Information) reveal characteristic peaks of MoS<sub>2</sub> (E<sup>1</sup><sub>2&nbsp;g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> peaks) and carbon (D and G peaks), further confirming the formation of MoS<sub>2</sub> and carbon material (detailed discussion in Supporting Information). The difference between the E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> peak can be used as ancillary support for the presence of monolayer carbon, with a value of 28.2 cm<sup>−1</sup> (&gt;6 layers) for pure MoS<sub>2</sub> and a value of ∼ 20.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to monolayer MoS<sub>2</sub> for MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0023_R_d35498333e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This monolayer feature of MoS<sub>2</sub> is caused by the monolayer carbon between adjacent MoS<sub>2</sub>, which shields the strong van der Waals interaction of MoS<sub>2</sub> layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0023_R_d35498333e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0024_R_d35498333e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>We also conduct X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS) analysis on the samples, and all expected elements can be observed in the corresponding XPS spectra for each sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In Fe 2p spectra of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1p</a>), the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks of FeMoS phase, located at 707.9 and 720.5&nbsp;eV, respectively, clearly indicate the effective Fe doping into MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0025_R_d35498333e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electron localization function (ELF) analyses demonstrate that the bonding between Fe and S atoms represents a coexistence of both ionic and covalent bonds (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) indicates that the Fe content in the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C samples increases with the addition of 2(C<sub>11</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>).Fe during the sample preparation process. In addition, high-resolution XPS spectra of Mo 3d, S 2p, C 1s, N 1s, and O 1s confirm the existence of MoS<sub>2</sub> and co-doping of N and O within carbon matrix (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a–e</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). In electron paramagnetic resonances (EPR) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0001">1q</a>), the intensity of characteristic peak (g = 2.003) strengthens with the increase in Fe doping concentration, indicating that Fe doping leads to the enrichment of non-intrinsic defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0026_R_d35498333e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These defects produced by Fe doping can induce the formation of localized electrons, accelerate ion diffusion and electron transfer, and provide extra active sites for ion storage. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and elemental analysis (EA) are conducted to detect the composition of samples, as presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. According to the EA results (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), the N, O co-doped carbon content in MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, and FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III is 0, 17.7, 21.3, 26.5, and 33.8 wt.%, respectively. The residues mass of MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, and FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III after heating in air can be calculated to 88.6, 74.4, 68.3, 58.5, and 43.9 wt.%, respectively, and exhibit similar results with TGA curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15f</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404263-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404263-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Ion Storage Performances in Half Cells</h3>
            
            <p>We subsequently present direct experimental evidence that the huge structural advantages of Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC bring about superior sodium storage performances. The first sodiation/desodiation profiles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2a</a>) at 0.1 C, cycling curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2b</a>) at 0.1 C, and rate curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2c</a>) of obtained samples are tested. It can be seen that pure MoS<sub>2</sub> shows the lowest first charge capacity (277.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), the fastest capacity fading (29.2%, capacity retention, 100 cycles), and the poorest rate performance (19.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 20 C). After constructing MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC structure, the sample (MoS<sub>2</sub>/C) shows an obvious improvement in the initial charge capacity (430.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), capacity retention (96.6%) after 100 cycles, and rate performance (107.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 20 C). Importantly, with the successful construction of Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC, there is a significant enhancement in the first charge capacity (532.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I, 700.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, 609.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III) and rate capability (190.0/341.0/146.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C for FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-I/FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II/FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-III, respectively). Furthermore, among these samples, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II demonstrates the highest reversible capacity (680.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and the highest capacity retention (97.2%) after 100 cycles (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the dissolution experiments of electrode materials in the electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information) also imply the high stability of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II. Moreover, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II exhibits the best rate performances among these samples. This is primarily ascribed to its higher EC (38.9 S cm<sup>−1</sup>, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), larger interlayer distance (11.6 Å) of MoS<sub>2</sub>, bigger specific surface area (84.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), and lower charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2d</a>). In addition to evaluating the rate performance at 25 °C, we conduct further tests to assess the rate capabilities of these electrodes over a wide temperature range from −40 to 60 °C. The results indicate that FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II consistently exhibits the best rate capability across this temperature range (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404263-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/535ca960-503e-4435-9bbd-ca0e8800b23a/adfm202404263-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/535ca960-503e-4435-9bbd-ca0e8800b23a/adfm202404263-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8ce2dbfe-a654-43ed-940c-5b1116202a5c/adfm202404263-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/535ca960-503e-4435-9bbd-ca0e8800b23a/adfm202404263-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404263-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404263" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of all the obtained materials at 25 °C: a) First charge/discharge curves, b) Cycling stability at 0.1 C, c) Rate performances, and d) Nyquist curves. Electrochemical performances of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II at −40–60 °C: e) Voltage profiles of the first cycle, f) cycling stability at 0.1 C, g) rate performances. h) Nyquist curves after rate test, i) potential response, j) <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> variation during GITT measurement, and k) cycling curves at 10 C. l) Comparison of rate performance of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II with previous reports on SIBs anodes at low operating temperatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Given the superior performance of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II among these samples, further evaluation of its electrochemical performance at a broad operating temperature range is conducted. Ether-based electrolytes are selected based on the experiment results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information) and excellent sodium ion storage performance at low temperatures in previous literature reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0028_R_d35498333e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0032_R_d35498333e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2e</a> displays the first charge-discharge profiles at different temperatures. In comparison to the first charge capacity of 700.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> obtained at 25 °C, the capacity (722.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at 60 °C is slightly higher, primarily attributed to the elevated operating temperature reducing the diffusion barrier for Na ions within the electrode to result in more full sodiation reaction. Despite the fact that lower operating temperatures tend to increase the diffusion barrier for Na ions, resulting in significant capacity loss,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0027_R_d35498333e1295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Energy">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode still delivers a remarkable high capacity of 575.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultra-low temperature of −40 °C, equivalent to 82.1% of its capacity at 25 °C. In addition, as the operating temperature decreases from 25 to −40 °C, an increase in polarization and a decline in voltage plateau are observed. This is primarily attributed to the elevated viscosity of the electrolyte and the increased impedance at the electrode/electrolyte interface at lower temperatures, resulting in reduced ionic conductivity and, consequently, heightened polarization and a lowered discharge voltage plateau.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0033_R_d35498333e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0034_R_d35498333e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ChemComm">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following 100 cycles at −40, 25, and 60 °C, the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrodes display no significant capacity loss, confirming its robust cycling stability over wide operation temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2f</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2g</a> exhibits the rate performance of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrodes at −40–60 °C. The electrode demonstrates similar and outstanding rate capacities at both 25 and 60 °C. Even when operating in the extremely low temperature of −40 °C, the electrode continues to maintain remarkable rate capabilities. Specifically, the electrode delivers a rate capacity of 379.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C, which is equivalent to 70.5% of the capacity obtained at 25 °C (538.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Raising the current density to 20 C, a high rate capacity of 177.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be obtained at −40 °C. Moreover, the capacity can be fully restored upon resetting the current density to 0.1 C. The low values of the R<sub>ct</sub> operating at different temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2h</a>) are the first reason behind the exceptional rate performance of the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode across a wide temperature range.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To further demonstrate the superiority of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode on Na-ion transport, we employ the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) to obtain the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) at the operation temperatures of −40, 25, and 60 °C. The equation for calculating <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0035_R_d35498333e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Sci.">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0036_R_d35498333e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Carbon Energy">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202404263-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6ba316ea-0ec3-462a-9eaf-fee34bb694ed/adfm202404263-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404263:adfm202404263-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202404263-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>

                                    <mi>τ</mi>

                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>M</mi>

                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}{{\left( {\frac{{{{m}_B}{{V}_M}}}{{{{M}_B}S}}} \right)}^2}\left( {\frac{{\Delta {{E}_S}}}{{\Delta {{E}_\tau }}}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>M<sub>B</sub></i>, <i>m<sub>B</sub></i>, and <i>V<sub>M</sub></i> are molar mass, mass, and molar volume of the active material, respectively. <i>S</i>, <i>τ</i>, <i>ΔE<sub>S</sub></i>, and <i>ΔE<sub>τ</sub></i> represent electrode area, constant current time, and voltage change, as well as total voltage change, respectively. The values of <i>τ</i>, <i>ΔE<sub>S</sub></i>, and <i>ΔE<sub>τ</sub></i> are determined from the GITT curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2i</a>). According to equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202404263-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>), the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values for FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrodes at −40, 25, and 60 °C can be calculated to be 2.5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>-6.3&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>, 3.8&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>-8.2&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>, and 2.4&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>-2.6&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2j</a>). These <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values surpass the majority of previously reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based SIB anodes, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). Particularly noteworthy is the fact that the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at −40 °C is only one order of magnitude lower than that at room temperature (25 °C), indicating the advantageous impact of the designed Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC structure on sodium ion transport at low temperatures. The exceptionally high <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values are the second reason behind the outstanding rate performance of the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrodes across a broad temperature range. In addition to its outstanding sodium ion transport capabilities, the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode also exhibits a long cycle life under repeated fast charging and discharging at 2 C over wide operation temperatures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). When testing at 10 C, FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode still maintains high capacity retentions of 84.1%, 83.8%, and 73.5% after 3000 cycles at operating temperatures of −40, 25, and 60 °C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2k</a>). The decrease in capacity retention rate with increasing operating temperature is primarily attributed to the accelerated decomposition of the electrolyte at high temperatures, leading to a degradation in battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0037_R_d35498333e1495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to indicate the advantages of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II, we compare the obtained performances with recent reports on SIB anode materials at different temperatures (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11–S14</a>, Supporting Information), which can conclude that the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II has significant advantages on capacity, cycling life, and fast-charging capability, especially for rate capability at low operating temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0002">2l</a>). In addition, the results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information) also demonstrate the excellent electrochemical performances of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II at high mass loading.</div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404263-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404263-sec-0050-title">2.3 Mechanism Analysis of Fast Sodium Ion Transport over Wide Operation Temperatures</h3>
            
            <p>To reveal the remarkable sodium storage performance of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode over a wide operation temperature range, we conduct in situ TEM, ex situ TEM, in situ magnetometry, ex situ XRD, and ex situ XPS on the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrodes. In situ TEM is conducted to observe real-time structural evolution of the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode upon sodiation at room temperature. The diagram for the in situ TEM observation is depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3a</a>, in which a selected FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II connected to the Cu half-grid serves as working electrode, Na<sub>2</sub>O as electrolyte, and a piece of metallic Na on W tip works as counter electrode. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3b,c</a>, and Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) show that FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II undergoes a very fast structural transformation within 19 s, which directly demonstrates the fast-charging capability of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II. From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3d</a>, it is evident that the layered Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>-NOC undergoes a conversion during sodiation into numerous ultra-small nanocrystals with a size of ≈2&nbsp;nm, which are distributed throughout the electrode. Two distinct lattice fringes with d-spacings of ≈0.22 and 0.16&nbsp;nm can be detected (insets of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3d</a>), corresponding to the (110) and (103) crystal planes of metallic Mo<sup>0</sup> and Fe<sup>0</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, the SAED pattern after full sodiation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3e</a>) clearly displays the diffraction rings of Mo<sup>0</sup> (110), (220) (JCPDS:42-1120), Fe<sup>0</sup> (103), (101) (JCPDS: 50–1275), and Na<sub>2</sub>S (200), (422) (JCPDS: 23–0441) to confirm the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S, metallic Mo<sup>0</sup>, and metallic Fe<sup>0</sup>, indicating that the conversion-type sodium storage mechanism of the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II. EDS elemental mapping images show the formed nanocrystals are uniformly dispersed into electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, in situ XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), ex situ XRD and XPS are also conducted to investigate the structural evolution of the FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II electrode during charge-discharge cycles, in which the electrodes cycled at 25&nbsp;°C as a representative are studied. From XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a,b</a>, Supporting Information), the diffraction peaks of (002) crystal planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> gradually transfer toward low angle when discharge from open circuit voltage to 1&nbsp;V attributed to intercalation reaction (xNa<sup>+</sup> + xe<sup>−</sup> + FeMoS<sub>2</sub>→Na<sub>x</sub>FeMoS<sub>2</sub>). Obviously, a weak diffraction peak at ∼ 38.9° arising from (220) crystal plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS: 23–0441) appears at 0.8&nbsp;V, along with the disappearance of the diffraction peaks of MoS<sub>2</sub>, suggesting the occurrence of conversion reaction ((4-x)Na<sup>+</sup> + (4-x)e<sup>−</sup> + Na<sub>x</sub>FeMoS<sub>2</sub>→Fe + Mo + 2Na<sub>2</sub>S). The intensity of the Na<sub>2</sub>S peak continues to increase from 0.8 to 0.5&nbsp;V to suggest the ongoing conversion reaction within the voltage range, and is almost unchanged from 0.5 to 0.01&nbsp;V, indicating the end of conversion reaction at ∼ 0.5&nbsp;V and the co-existence of Mo, Fe, and Na<sub>2</sub>S within the voltage range. The absence of the diffraction peaks of Mo and Fe crystals is attributed to their ultrasmall particle size as observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3d</a>. The above results indicate that the intercalation reaction occurs about from open circuit voltage to 1&nbsp;V and the conversion reaction occurs about from 1 to 0.5&nbsp;V. During the charging process, the intensity of Na<sub>2</sub>S peak remains unchanged from 0.01 to 1&nbsp;V, reduces at 1.5&nbsp;V, and disappears at 2.3&nbsp;V, indicating that oxidation reaction occurs about from 1 to 2.3&nbsp;V. With the disappearance of the diffraction peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S, no obvious new diffraction peaks appear, which may be due to the ultrasmall size or amorphous nature of the oxidation reaction products. To reveal clarity about this, ex situ XPS spectra of Mo 3d and Fe 2p are tested when discharge to 0.01 and charge to 3&nbsp;V. Clearly, in the Mo 3d (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24c</a>, Supporting Information) and Fe 2p (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24d</a>, Supporting Information) spectra, the characteristic peaks (229.2&nbsp;eV, Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub>; 232.3&nbsp;eV, Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>; 707.7&nbsp;eV, Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) of the pristine electrode notably shift toward lower binding energy (228.3&nbsp;eV, Mo<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub>; 231.1&nbsp;eV, Mo<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub>; 707.3&nbsp;eV, Fe<sup>0</sup>) after discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0038_R_d35498333e1659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a>-<a href="#adfm202404263-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404263-bib-0041_R_d35498333e1659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Commun.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggests that not only the Mo elements convert into Mo<sup>0</sup> as previously reported, but also the Fe elements doped into the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice undergo transformation into Fe<sup>0</sup> during the deep sodiation process, which show similar results with TEM observations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404263-fig-0003">3d,e</a>). When charging to 3&nbsp;V, the peaks of Mo<sup>0</sup> 3d still exist (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24c</a>, Supporting Information), which indicates that the Mo nanocrystals are not oxidized. The result indicates that Mo and S cannot reversibly return to MoS<sub>2</sub> during the oxidation reaction. Differently, the peaks of Fe 2p shift toward higher binding energies from 707.3&nbsp;eV for Fe<sup>0</sup> to 710.1&nbsp;eV for Fe<sup>2+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24d</a>, Supporting Information), which indicates that the Fe<sup>0</sup> nanocrystals formed during the conversion reaction are oxidized into Fe<sup>2+</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404263-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca2598a2-468b-46d7-af6b-6eeefd00b233/adfm202404263-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>

                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ TEM and magnetometry characterizations of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II at 25 °C: a) The schematic diagram of in situ TEM device, b) TEM image before sodiation, c,d) TEM images after full sodiation, e) SAED pattern after full sodiation, and f) In situ magnetometry. Ex situ TEM and in situ magnetometry characterizations of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II at −40 °C: g) HRTEM image after full sodiation, h) SAED pattern after full sodiation, and i) In situ magnetometry. Ex situ TEM and in situ magnetometry characterizations of FeMoS<sub>2</sub>/C-II at 60 °C: j) HRTEM image after full sodiation, k) SAED pattern after full sodiation, and l) In situ magnetometry.</div>
